        <td style="width: 302px; height: 250px; vertical-align: top;">
            <div class="catchall300" style='width:300px; overflow:hidden;float:right;'><!-- RoS_300x250 --> <div id ='div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13' style='width:300px; height:250px; overflow:hidden;'><script type='text/javascript'>if ($('#div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13').is(':visible')) {googletag.cmd.push(function () { googletag.display('div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13'); });}</script></div></div>

        <tr id="so4" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Martinez was one of the few bright spots on a poor Astros team last season. The jump from Double-A didn’t seem to bother him, as he hit .274/.319/.423 in 226 plate appearances last season. It wouldn’t kill him to take a few more walks, but his Minor League slash line of .342/.407/.551 is certainly encouraging. With his 2011 performance all but ensuring him a starting role in 2012, Martinez looks like a solid bet to improve if he can continue to adjust to Major League pitching. (Chris Cwik)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Martinez looks like a solid breakout candidate next season. If he can raise his walk rate and show a little more thump, he’ll definitely have value in mixed leagues. The fact that he plays for the Astros should keep him under that radar as drafts approach.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so5" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>J.D. Martinez is currently slated to be the Astros' starting left fielder in 2013, and given Houston's lack of talent on the major league level, he is probably going to get a lot of playing time there. That alone should get your attention, particularly in NL-only leagues. However, while he should be drafted in most NL-only leagues, he's still a low-end option. Although he was seen as a sleeper going into 2012, he simply hit terribly. One cannot really pin it on random variation in batted ball luck, either. Martinez walks at a slightly above-average rate, but that's not enough to make up for below-average contact rate and power. The 2011 numbers that got him attention are not all that impressive, either, other than a higher batting average on balls in play. Martinez will be 25 at the beginning of the 2013 season, and while that is young, it is not that young. There is some upside here, so he's definitely worth a look over older low-end options. Do not let him go undrafted, but do not expect a budding superstar, either. Martinez being an average corner outfielder in 2013 would be something of a surprise. (Matt Klaassen)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Martinez is somewhat young, and there is some upside there. However, he has shown very little in terms of fantasy potential in more than 600 major league plate appearances. He is a low-end option with a chance of being a bit more.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so6" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Martinez suffered through an injury-marred season, but failed to display any type of solid offense while he was on the field. His walk rate plummeted, strikeout rate spiked and his power was worse than league average. With little speed, it all combines for a package not very attractive to fantasy owners. There have been some encouraging reports that the work he's doing on his swing is working, but he went unclaimed in the Rule 5 draft. And the Astros' outfield carousel has better options, which leaves Martinez without a starting gig. At best, fantasy owners could expect some cheap middling power from a reserve outfielder. (<a href="https://twitter.com/MikePodhorzer" target="_blank">Mike Podhorzer</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Injuries and poor offense cost Martinez a starting outfield job and the Astros found better alternatives to replace him. With mediocre power and the need to make better contact, there is little upside for fantasy owners to hang their hats on.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so7" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Martinez was a great story in 2014, using the offseason to rework his swing and turning himself into one of the best hitters in the league. Between Triple-A and the majors, J.D. hit .314 with 33 homers and 98 RBIs, all in 551 plate appearances. With his contact rate being a bit on the low side, he may not continue hitting at such a high average in 2015. However, he hits the ball ridiculously hard, so his batting average on balls in play may not regress as much as you might expect. Peg him for a .290 average with 25-30 home runs, while playing close to average defense in the outfield this season. If he continues to make adjustments to his swing and approach, he could keep hitting at the .300 level and even add in some more walks as pitchers need to adjust to him. 2014 will not be the last year J.D. is a force to be reckoned with. (Dan Farnsworth)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>A breakout 2014 might have some thinking a return to past production is coming this year, but they would be wrong. Martinez is a new hitter, and he will be one of the best power sources in the league.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so8" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Martinez was an easy regression candidate last year after a breakout season that saw him surge across the board, including a 110-point jump in isolated slugging percentage and a gaudy .389 batting average on balls in play that helped fuel his .315 average. The BABIP regression did come, but that stat dropped just 50 points and he still managed a healthy .282 batting average. Meanwhile, the ISO continued upward with another career high, this time a .253 mark. Even his proponents saw more of an upper-20s homer output, but Martinez's overhaul to his swing proved legitimate and he has now spent the last two seasons as one of the preeminent power threats in the game. He's in a bit of the Nelson Cruz mold with this late breakout, though he only needed one team change. Like Cruz, many will be waiting for a Martinez fall, but since his breakout, injury and suspension have been about the only things capable of slowing Cruz. Where I could see Martinez veering from the Cruz path is batting average. JDM has a bigger swing-and-miss component in his game that leaves a lot of pressure on his BABIP to maintain a plus batting average. Martinez has 26% and 27% strikeout rates in his two great seasons, while Cruz's 25% last season was a full season high. The tradeoff in AVG upside is that Martinez is seven years younger than Cruz; these two shouldn't be drafted far apart and not just because of their similar path to stardom. (<a href="http://thespguide.com/">Paul Sporer</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Last year in this space, Dan Farnsworth enthusiastically backed Martinez and those who listened were rewarded with a 38-HR season. JDM looks to be evolving into one of the game's best sluggers in the midst of his prime.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>I mean, I guess Martinez's 2016 season could be perceived as a letdown. He hit "only" 22 home runs  — a three-year low, both nominally and per plate appearance — and barely eclipsed 500 plate appearances. Yet last year also offered positive developments: his strikeout and walk rates improved for a third straight season, and his hard and soft-contact rates ranked ninth-best and third-best, respectively, among qualified hitters. The excellent contact quality has lofted his batting average on balls in play to impressive levels - he trails only Paul Goldschmidt in BABIP since 2014. He recaptured his elite BABIP from 2014 at the expense of  his fly ball gains from 2015. This correlation should come as no surprise; <a href="http://www.fangraphs.com/fantasy/new-hitter-xbabip-based-on-bis-batted-ball-data/">xBABIP</a> shows that a seven-point increase in fly balls correlates with a 35- to 40-point decrease in BABIP. In other words, his actual BABIP has behaved exactly how we would expect it to each of the last three years. It becomes a question, then, of which version of Martinez you like better: 25-homer/.310 version or the 35-homer/.280 version. (Both sounds pretty dang good if you ask me.) Although durability could now be a concern, he's a top-12 outfielder when healthy and should be treated as such. Just don't expect us to ever properly hyperlink his name. (Alex Chamberlain)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>He endured an injury-shortened 2016 season, but it wasn't anything out of the ordinary for the spectacular Martinez. He's a unique power-average dual threat and a no-brainer top-12 outfielder.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
    </tr>
</table>
